reminiscence of european travel 1815-1819 by major we frye edit with a preface and note by salomon reinach member of the institute of france london 1908 to vam sr preface the knowledge of major frye 's manuscript and the privilege of publish it for the first time i owe to the kindness of two french lady the miss g -- they father a well know artist and critic use to spend the summer month at saint germain-en-laye together with he wife who be a english woman by birth they have be for a long time intimately acquaint with major frye who live and end he life in that quiet town the major 's hostess mme de w -- after he death in 1858 bring the manuscript to mr g -- and give it to she in memory of she friend it be duly preserve in the g -- family but remain unnoticed the miss g -- rediscover it in 1907 when it have be lie in a cupboard for upward of half a century on they show it to I i think it be interesting for many reason and worthy of introduction to the public i hope the reader will share my opinion which be also that of several english scholar and man of letter to whom i communicate extract from the manuscript the reminiscence be in the form of letter address to a correspondent who however be never name and of whose health family and private circumstance not the slightest mention be to be find so i be incline to believe that he never exist and that major frye choose to imitate president de bross and other who thus record they travel experience in epistolary form the manuscript -- which will eventually be deposit in a public library -- be entirely in major frye 's large and legible hand at some later time it be evidently revise by himself but many name which i have endeavor to complete be leave in blank or only indicate by initial there be three folio volume bind in paper board in this edition it have be think advisable to leave out a certain number of page devote to theatricals of which major frye be a great votary and also some lengthy description of landscape museum and church the interest of which to modern reader do not correspond to the space occupy by they for the information contain in the footnote i be indebted to many correspondent english french swiss belgian and italian to whom i here express my hearty thanks i be under special obligation to sir charle dilke mr oscar browning professor novatus professor corrado riccus commandant espérandieu professor cumont professor still and mr höchberg major frye 's tombstone be in the cemetery of saint germain and read thus : to the memory of major william edward frye who depart this life the 9th day of October 1858 on the same stone have be add in french : perceval edmond litchfield décédé le 15 avril 1888 about pe litchfield i know nothing he must have be the major 's intimate friend during the last period of he life \* \* \* \* \* we frye be bear oct 29 1784 and receive he education at eton -lrb- 1797-9 -rrb- in the time of the french revolution the system be he say to drill into the head of the boy strong aristocratic principle and hatred of democracy and of the french in particular the effect produce on the youth be the reverse of that intend from 1799 to 1822 he belong to the british army : here be a abstract of he service : ensign 2nd foot 5th August 1799 lieutenant 2nd foot 7th march 1800 half-pay 4th foot 14th April 1808 lieutenant 24th foot 8th December 1804 captain 56th foot 18th April 1805 3rd ceylon regt 15th feb 1810 half-pay 3rd foot 7th march 1816 4th foot 24th feb 1820 brevet-major 12th August 1819 sell out 15th August 1822 in 1799 frye take a part in the british expedition to holland in 1801 he be in egypt with lord abercrombie 's army and receive the medal for war service he career in indium last six year and give he occasion to visit the three presidency and ceylon in 1814 he return on furlough to europe and be in brussels during the waterloo campaign the subsequent year -- 1815 to 1819 -- he employ visit western europe as appear from he reminiscence i have read letter of he which prove that he live in pari from 1830 to 1832 later about 1848 he take a apartment in saint germain and die there in 1858 major frye be a very distinguished linguist besides know greek and latin he understand almost all european language and be capable of write correctly in french italian and german the miss g -- have show I a rare book publish by he at paris in 1844 under the follow title : trois chants de l'edda vaftrudnismal thrymsquidal skirnisfor traduit en ver français accompagnés de note explicative des mythes et allégories et suivis d'autres poèmes par we frye ancien major d'infanterie au service d'angleterre membre de l'académie des arcadiens de rome se vend à paris pour l'auteur chez heideloff & cie libraire 18 rue des filles st thomas 1844 -lrb- in 8vo xii 115 pp -rrb- at the end of that volume be translation by major frye of several northern poem -- in german italian and english verse -- from the danish and the swedish then come two sonnet in french verse the one in honor of lafayette the other about the duke of orléans whose premature death he compare with that of the northern hero of the edda balder a part of frye 's translation of the edda before appear in book form have be publish in _ l'echo de la littérature et des beaux art _ a periodical edit by the major 's friend m de belenet frye love poetry though he idea on the subject be rather those of the eighteenth century than we own it be interesting to find a english officer reading voltaire gessner ariosto and quote they from memory -lrb- which explain that some of he quotation have to be correct -rrb- the sentimental vein of rousseau 's generation still flow and vibrate in he as when he say that he have never be able to read the letter of wolmar to st preux in rousseau 's _ nouvelle héloïse _ without shed tear german minor poetry now quite forget attract he almost as much as the great page of schiller bürger and goethe the miss g possess a manuscript translation in three volume in the major 's own hand of wieland 's _ agathodemon _ do into english this he evidently intend to publish as he have write the title-page which be word as follow : agathodemon a philosophical romance translate from the german of wieland by we frye member of the academy deglus arcadus in rome and of the royal society of northern antiquarian of copenhagen ex-major of infantry in he british majesty 's service frye describe with accuracy and show much appreciation of fine scenery and architecture he judgement in painting and sculpture be sincere though often betray the autodidact and amateur he love music especially rossinus 's opera which be then begin they long career of triumph theatricals of all sort especially ballet have a great attraction for he and elicit he enthusiastic comment in compare tragedy and comedy which he have see perform in different country he give repeat proof of he knowledge and critical insight we can take he as a good example of that intelligent class of english traveller whose intercourse with the continental _ litteratus _ have so well contribute to establish the good reputation of british culture and refine appreciation of the art the chief interest of frye 's reminiscence lie however in quite another direction he be a friend of liberty a friend of france a admirer of napoleon and a hater of the tory régime which bring about napoleon 's downfall france 's attempt at european domination in the napoleonic era be graciously describe as but so many effort towards spread the light of civilization over europe these word write about a quite recent work and à propo of the entente cordiale apply perfectly to frye 's reminiscence travel immediately before and after the emperor 's collapse he find that everywhere except in tuscany the french domination be regret because the ideal of liberty and equality have shine and vanish with the tricolor flag he admire the french people though not the _ ultra _ and bigot and have fine word of praise for the french army : yes the french soldier be a fine fellow i have serve against they in holland and in egypt and i will never flinch from render justice to they exemplary conduct and lofty valor he take trouble to refute the exaggerated report which be then circulate all over europe about the cruelty and vandalism practice by the french : if the french since the revolution have not always fight for liberty they have do so invariably for science and wherever they carry they victorious arm abuse be abolish amelioration of all kind follow and the art of life be improve we government since the accession of george iii have never raise its arm except in favor of old abuse to uphold despotism and unfair privilege or to establish commercial monopoly sometimes indeed speak of he own country and its government major frye use very hard word which might seem unpatriotic if we do not know from many other memoirs and letter to what a terrible strain orthodox toryism couple with bigotry and hypocrisy have put the patience of liberal englishman at that period he call the british government the most dangerous artful and determine enemy of all liberty -- england he say have be always ready to lend a hand to crush liberty to perpetuate abuse and to rivet the fetter of monarchical feudal and ecclesiastical tyranny and later on he inveigh against the english merchant who contribute with they gold to uphold the corrupt system of pitt and to carry on unjust unreasonable and liberticide war whatever may be the final judgement of history on the tory principle in politics in the day of the congress of vienna major frye 's love of liberty and intellectual progress entitle he to the sympathy of those who share he generous feeling and do not consider that personal freedom and individual rights be article for home use only since frye write the whole of europe excepting perhaps russium have reap the benefit of the french revolution and reduce if not suppress what the major call kingcraft and priestcraft he do not attempt to divine the future but the history of europe in the nineteenth century have be largely in accordance with he desire and hope it be not a small merit for a writer in the midst of one of the most rabid reaction that the world have know to have cling with such tenacity to ideal the complete victory of which may -- return to england \* \* \* \* \* chapter i may-june 1815 passage from ceylon to england -- napoleon 's return -- ostend -- bruge -- ghent -- the king of france at mass -- alost -- bruxelle -- the duke of wellington very confident -- feeling of the belgian -- good conduct of british troops -- monument in bruxelle -- theatricals -- genappe and namur -- complaint against the prussian troops -- mon -- major-general adam -- tournay -- a french deserter -- general clinton 's division -- cavalry review -- the duke de berrus -- back to bruxelle -- unjust opinion about napoleon and the french -- battle at ligny -- the day of waterloo in bruxelle -- visit to the battlefield -- terrible condition of the wound -- kindness of the bruxelloi bruxelle may 1 1815 i proceed to the fulfilment of my promise to give you from time to time the detail of my tour and my reflection on the circumstance that occur at this momentous crisis to I who have spend the greatest part of my life out of europe the whole scene be so new that i be quite bewildered with it and you will i be afraid as i write on the impulse of the moment find my idea at time rather incoherently put together what change have take place in europe within the last two year and how great be those which occur during the interval of my passage from ceylon last year which island i quit about the time that we receive in that part of the world intelligence of the battle of leipsic have have a long passage from distant taprobane it be only on my arrival at the cape of good hope that i learn to my utter astonishment the news of the capitulation of pari to the ally power and of the overthrow of the power and dynasty of napoleon i recollect that at the cape there be great rejoicing and jubilee on this occasion but i confess as to myself i do not see any reason for give vent to this extravagant joy and i must have have even at that time somehow or other a presentiment of what would soon happen as in communicate this intelligence to a friend in indium i make use of these word : get a court dress make my good friend and a big wig ruffle shirt and hair-powder and stick a old-fashioned sword by you side for depend on it old fashion will come into play again the most arbitrary and aristocratic notion will be revive and terrible machination will be frame against the liberty of europe of course at the cape we only hear one side of the question and i begin to be almost convince that it be as necessary for humanity as for the repose of europe that the giant should be put down and i be console when it be effect ostensibly at least by the voice of the people i have scarcely be three month in england when the return of napoleon from elba and the extraordinary dislocation of the bourbon from the throne of france summon europe again to arm the crusade be preach at vienna and behold he grace of wellington appoint the godfrey of the holy league i have reason about six week before the news of this event reach london from some conversation i have with a intelligent friend who have just return from a tour on the continent to suppose that the slightest combination against the bourbon would prove successful from they injudicious conduct and from the temper of the people but i never could have suppose that the return of the man of elba would be hail with such unparalleled and unanimous acclamation as i have long ago wish for a opportunity of visit the continent of europe which have never before occur to I i eagerly embrace the offer make to I by my friend major-general wilson formerly lieut-governor of ceylon -lrb- 1 -rrb- to accompany he on a military tour through the country about to be the theater of war though i have never before visit the continent -lrb- except with the british army in the invasion of holland in 1799 when i begin my military career -rrb- yet i be not wholly unprepared for travel have unite to a classical as well as military education a tolerable knowledge of history and a partial acquirement of the principal modern european language which i have begin to learn when very young and which i keep up during my leisure hour in indium which like those of don quixote be many i prefer this study infinitely to that of the asiatic language for which i never feel any taste as i dislike bombast hyperbole and exaggeration and though a ardent admirer of the muse i never could find pleasure in what voltaire term le bon style oriental ou l'on fait danser les montagnes et les colline and i prefer the amatory effusion of ovid to those of the great king solomon himself the war will no doubt commence in belgium and of course the emperor napoleon will be the assailant for it can not be suppose that after the act of ban pass against he by the amphictyon of vienna he will remain tranquil and not strike the first blow which may render he master of belgium and its resource we embark at ramsgate on the first of may for ostend on board of a small vessel bind thither we fellow passenger be two officer of dragoon several commissary with they servant horse etc after a passage of twenty-four hour we enter the harbor of ostend at one o'clock the follow day ostend once so flourishing and opulent have long since fall into decay its usual dullness be however just now interrupt by the bustle of troops land to join the ally army cavalry infantry artillery horse gun store etc be land every minute the quay be the only part of this city which can boast of handsome building the fortification seem to be much out of repair in fact the aggrandizement of antwerp occasion necessarily the deterioration of ostend the general and myself go to put up at the _ tête d'or _ the only inn where we could procure bed and we embark early next morning at the embouchure of the canal on board of a _ treckschuyt _ which convey we in three hour to bruge the landscape between ostend and bruge be extremely monotonous it be a uniformly flat country yet it be please to the eye at this season of the year from the verdure of the plain which be all appropriate to pasturage and from the appearance of the different village and town of which the eye can embrace a considerable number there be a good road on the bank of the canal and the troops on they line of march enliven much the scene bruge formerly the grand mart and emporium of the commerce of the east not only for the low country but for all the north of europe seem if we may judge from the state of the building and the stillness that prevail to be also in a state of decline we however have only time to visit the _ hotel de ville _ and to remark the immense height of the steeple on the _ grande place _ we observe a number of pretty woman in the street and in the shop employ in lace make bruge have be at all time renown for the beauty of the female sex and this bring to my recollection a passage in schiller 's tragedy of the _ maid of orlean _ wherein the duke of burgundy say that the greatest boast of bruge be the beauty of its woman another _ treckschuyt _ be to start at twelve o'clock for ghent but we prefer go by land and general wilson hire a carriage for that purpose the distance be about thirty mile the road from bruge to ghent or gand be perfectly straight line with tree and pave like a street the country be quite flat and though there be nothing to bind the horizon the tree on each side of the road intercept the view we arrive at ghent about six in the afternoon of the 4th and have some difficulty in find room as the different hotel be fill with officer of the ally army but at length after many ineffectual application at several we obtain admission at the _ hotel de flandre _ where we take possession of a double-bedded room the only one unoccupied gand seem to be a very neat clean and handsome city with a air of magnificence about it the _ grande place _ be very striking and the promenade be align with tree we inspect the exterior of several public building and visit the interior of several church in the cathedral we have the honor of see at high mass he most christian majesty monsieur and the comte de blacas vicomte de chateaubriand and other compose the court of _ notre père de gand _ as loui xviii be humorously term by the french from he have fix he head-quarter here a great many french officer who have follow he fortune be also here but they seem principally to belong to the garde du corps a number of military attend the service in the cathedral in order to witness the devotions of the bourbon family monsieur have all the appearance of a wear out debauchee and to see he with a missal in he hand and the strange contrite face he assume be truly ridiculous these prince instigate no doubt by the priest make a great parade of they sanctity for which however those who be acquaint with they character will not give they much credit but religious cant be the order of the day _ intra et extra iliacos muros _ abroad as well as in england the king of france take the lead have in view no doubt the advice of buckingham to richard iii : a pray ' r book in you hand my lord be well for on that ground i 'll make a holy descant and m de chateaubriand will no doubt trumpet forth the devotion and christian humility of he master those however who be at all acquaint with this prince 's habit and be not interested in palliating or conceal they insinuate that he devotions at the table be more sincere than at the altar and that like the giant margutte in the morgante maggiore of pulcus he place more faith and reliance on a cappone lesso ossium arrosto than on the consecrate but less substantial wafer -lrb- 2 -rrb- after contemplate this edifying spectacle we return to we inn and the next morning after breakfast we set out on we journey to bruxelle the road be exactly similar to that between bruge and gand but the country appear to be richer and more diversified and many country house be observable on the road side we pass thus several neat village at one o'clock we stop at alost to refresh we horse and dine at the table d'hôte be a number of french officer belong to the garde du corps on enter into conversation with one of they i find that he as well as several other of they have serve under napoleon and have even be patronise and promote by he but i suppose that be the son of the ancient _ noblesse _ they think that gratitude to a _ parvenu _ like he be rather too plebeian a virtue some of they however with whom i converse after dinner seem to regret the step they have take if we be successful say they it can only be by means of the ally army and who know what condition they may impose on france if we should be unsuccessful we be exile probably for life from we country during dinner two pretty look girl with musical instrument enter the hall and regale we ear with singing some romance among which be _ dunoi le troubadour _ and _ la sentinelle _ they sing with much taste and feeling i surmise this be not the only profession they exercise if i might judge from the _ doux yeux _ they occasionally direct to some of the officer these girl do not at least seem by they demeanor as if likely to incur the anathema of rinaldo in the _ orlando furioso _ : meritamente muoro una crudele but rather more disposed to dar vita all ` amator fidele -lrb- 3 -rrb- alost be a neat clean town or large village and the same description will serve for all the town and village in brabant and flander as they be build on the same plan we arrive at bruxelle late in the evening and put up at the _ hotel d'angleterre _ this morning the general and myself go to pay we respects to the _ gran capitano _ of the _ holy league _ and we leave we card he be i hear very confident of the result of the campaign and no doubt he have for he the prayer of all the pious in england against those atheistical fellow the french and these prayer will surely elicit a host of angel to come down to aid in the destruction of the pandemonium of pari where satan 's lieutenant sit enthrone the reflect people here be astonish that napoleon do not begin the attack the inhabitant of belgium be in general from all that i can hear or see not at all pleased with the present order of thing and they much lament the be sever from france the two people the belgian and hollander do not seem to amalgamate and the former though they render ample justice to the moderation good sense and beneficent intention of the present monarch who be personally respect by every one yet do not disguise they wish to be reunite to france and do not hesitate to avow they attachment to the emperor napoleon this union do not please the hollander either on other grounds they complain that they interest have be sacrifice entirely to those of the house of orange and they say that from the readiness they display in shake off the yoke of france and the great weight they thereby throw into the scale they be entitle to the restitution of all they colony in asia africa and america the colony of the cape of good hope and ceylon be what they most regret for these colony in particular furnished ample employment and the means of provision for the cadet of patrician family if you tell they they have acquire the belgic province as a indemnification they answer : so much the worse for we for now the patronage of the colonial office must be divide between we and the belgian the preparation for the grand conflict about to take place be carry on with unabating activity the conscription be rigorously enforce and every youth capable of bear arm be enrol almost all the officer of the belgian army and a great proportion of the soldiery have serve with the french and have be participator of they laurel one can not therefore suppose that they be actuate by any very devour zeal against they former commander nor have i find amongst the shop-keeper or respectable people with whom i have converse and who have be falsely represent as have suffer much from the tyranny of napoleon any who dislike either he person or government and certainly none either high or low express the cannibal wish that i hear some english country gentleman and london merchant utter for the destruction of pari and of the french people nor would it be easy to find here man of the _ humane _ and _ generous _ sentiment profess by some of we alderman and contractor when they welcome with ferocious acclamation of joy and be ready to embrace the baschkir or cossack who tell they that he have slaughter so many french with he own hand nor would the lady here be so eager to kiss old blucher as be the case in london this city be fill with british and hanoverian troops they conduct be exemplary nor be any complaint crime ignorance be never yet the safeguard of virtue as for myself i honor and esteem the scottish nation and i must say that i have find more liberal idea and more sound philosophy among individual either of sculpture of architecture or painting nor be i desirous of imitate the young englishman who in write to he father from italy describe so much in detail and so scientifically every production or staple peculiar to the city which he happen to visit that he write like a cheese-monger from parma like a silk mercer from leghorn like a olive and oil merchant from lucca like a picture dealer we leave brussels yesterday afternoon and have obtain passport to visit the military post we go to genappe a small village half-way between bruxelle and namur where we bring to for the night at a they a lieutenant in the 2nd west prussian regiment have the kindness to conduct we to see the field of battle where the french under jourdan defeat the austrian in 1794 it be at a very short distance from the town he explain the position of the two army in a manner perfectly clear and satisfactory to we the prussian officer all seem very eager for the commencement of hostility and they only fear be now that all these mighty preparation will end in nothing viz either that the french people alarm at the magnitude of the preparation against they will compel the emperor napoleon to abdicate or that the ally will grow cool and under the influence of austrium bring about a negotiation which may end in a recognition of the imperial title and dynasty they would compound for a defeat at first provide the war be likely to be prolong in the meantime reinforcement continue to arrive daily for they army we hear but little news of the intention or movement of the other ally it be forbid to from not know the tenor of we passport i go accordingly with the sergeant to this house there i find the officer command the piquet and several other sit at table carouse with beer and tobacco and nearly invisible from the cloud of smoke which pervade the room i explain to the officer who we be and request he to put on the passport he _ visa _ in the german language so that the non-commissioned be pay for extra and be generally at the price of three franc per bottle this hotel be very neatly fit up and be very near the _ hôtel de ville _ at the table d'hôte i frequently meet prussian officer who on come in to visit bruxelle put up here we have just learn the proceedings of the _ champ de mai _ at pari by which it appear that napoleon be solemnly recognize and confirm as emperor of the french this intelligence send a young prussian officer who sit next to I in a transport of joy for this make the war certain the prussian seem determine to revenge themselves for the humiliation they suffer from the french during the time they occupy they country and i sincerely pity by anticipation the fate of the french peasant upon whom these gentleman may chance to be quarter terrible will be the first shock of battle and it may be daily expect and dreadful will be the consequence to the poor inhabitant of the seat of war can not this war be avoid i be not politician enough to foresee the consequence of allow napoleon to keep quiet and undisturbed possession of the throne of france but the consequence of a defeat on the part of the ally will be the loss of belgium and the probable annihilation of the british army certainly the dissolution of the coalition for the minor german power and very likely austrium also would be induce to make a separate peace we can clearly see that napoleon have not now the power he formerly possess and that the republican party into whose hand he have throw himself seem disposed not only to remain at peace but to shackle he in every possible manner it be evident too that he last success be owe to the dislike of the people to the bourbon from they injudicious and treacherous conduct and the threat and impossible language hold by the priest and emigrant towards the holder of property pave the way for the success of he enterprise and enable he to achieve a triumph unparalleled in history on the contrary by force he to go to war should he gain the first victory belgium will be re-unite to france all the resource of that country bring into the scale against the ally napoleon will be more popular than ever the republican party will be put to silence the enthusiasm of the army will rise beyond all restraint and in a word napoleon will be himself again the other ally can do little without the assistance of england and we finances be by no means in a state to bear such intolerable drain as to the prussian on minute enquiry i do not find that they be so ill-treated by the french as be generally believe and that except the burden of have troops quarter on they -lrb- no small annoyance i allow -rrb- they have not much reason to complain the quartering of the troops on they and the payment of the war contribution be the necessary consequence of the occupation of they country by a enemy but i have just be read a german work write by a native of berlin shortly after the entry of the french troops in that city after the battle of jena in 1806 this work be entitle _ vertraute briefe aus berlin _ and in it the author distinctly declare that the discipline observe by the french troops during the occupation of berlin be highly strict and praiseworthy and that the few excess that take place be commit by the troops of the rhenish confederation and he add that the inhabitant prefer have a french soldier quarter on they to a westphalian bavarian or würtembergher further the troops that behave with the greatest oppression and insolence towards the burgher be those belong to a corps compose of native prussian raise for the service of napoleon by the prince of isenburg -lrb- 12 -rrb- in he recruit address the prince invite the prussian youth to enter into the service of the invincible napoleon and tell he that to the soldier of napoleon everything be permit the regiment be soon fit up and the soldier begin to put in practice in good earnest the theory of the _ affiche _ they commit excess of all sort and one officer in particular behave so brutally and infamously to a poor tailor on whom he be quarter and to whom before he enter the french service he be under the greatest obligation that general hulin the commandant of the place at berlin during the french occupation be oblige to cashier he publicly on the parade and to cause he epaulette to be tear from he coat in order to mark the disgust and indignation that he and all the french officer feel at the base ingratitude of this man this work vertraute briefe -lrb- confidential letter -rrb- contain much curious matter and very interesting anecdote respect the corruption venality and depravation that prevail in the prussian court and army previous to the war in 1806 let this suffice to show that the prussian have not so much reason to complain against the french as they pretend to have besides the conduct of the prussian government itself be so vacillate and contradictory that they have themselves only to blame for what they suffer they should have support austrium in 1805 but the fact be that the vanity and the _ amour propre _ of the prussian military be so hurt at the humiliation they experience at and after the battle of jena that it be this that have embittered they so much against the french let it not however be suppose for a moment that i seek to excuse or palliate the conduct of napoleon towards prussium i have always think it not only unjust but impolitic impolitic because prussium be and ought always to be the obvious and natural ally of france and napoleon instead of endeavor to crush that power should have aggrandize she and make she the paramount power in germany it be in fact he obvious policy to cede hanover in perpetuity to prussium and have render thereby the breach between the house of brandenburgh and hanover irreparable and irreconcilable this would have throw prussium necessarily into the arm of france in whose system she must then have move and all british influence on the continent would have be effectually put a end to another prime fault of napoleon be that he do not crush and dismember austrium in 1809 as he have it in he power to do and by so do he would have merit and obtain the thanks and good will of all germany for have overturn so despotic and light-fearing a government but he have pay dearly for these error instead of destroy a despotic power -lrb- austrium -rrb- he choose rather to crush a enlighten and liberal nation for such i esteem the prussian nation and i always separate the prussian people from they government the latter fell and fall unpitied after one battle but it have be almost miraculously restore by the unparalleled exertion and energy of the burgher and people may this be a lesson to the government and may the king of prussium not prove ungrateful troops continue to arrive here daily and now that the ceremony of the _ champ de mai _ be over we may expect that napoleon will repair to he army and commence operation June 17 napoleon arrive at maubeuge on the 18th and the grand conflict have begin the prussian be attack on the 14th and 15th at ligny and drive from they position -lrb- 13 -rrb- they be say to have suffer immense loss and to be retreat with the utmost confusion we turn come next the thunder of the cannon be hear here distinctly the most part of yesterday and some part of we army must have be engage we troops have all march out of bruxelle in the direction of the frontier in the affair with the prussian we learn that the duke of brunswick be kill and that blucher narrowly escape be make prisoner June 18 the grand conflict have begin with we it be now four o'clock pm the issue be not know the roar of the cannon continue unabated all be bustle confusion and uncertainty in this city car with wound be come in continually the general opinion be that we army will be compel to retreat to antwerp and it be even expect that the french will be in bruxelle to-night all the towns-people be on the rampart listen to the sound of the cannon this city have be in the greatest alarm and agitation since the 16th when a violent cannonade be hear during the afternoon from what i have be able to collect the french attack the prussian on the 14th and a desperate conflict take place on that day and the whole of the 15th -lrb- 14 -rrb- when the whole of the prussian army at ligny fleurus and charleroy be totally defeat and drive from its position a dislocation of we troops take place early in the morning of the 16th and we advanced guard consist of the highland brigade and two battalion of nassau-usingen fall in with the advanced guard of the french army command by marshal ney near quatre-bra and make such a gallant defense against he corps d'armée as to keep it in check the whole day and enable itself to fall back in good order to its present position with the rest of the army about ten mile in front of bruxelle indeed i be inform that nothing could exceed the admirable conduct of the corps above mention yesterday we hear no cannonade but this afternoon it have be unceasing and still continue all the caricature and satire against napoleon have disappear from the window and stall the shop be all shut the english family fly to antwerp and the proclamation of the baron de capellen -lrb- 15 -rrb- to the inhabitant wherein he exhort they to be tranquil and assure they that the bureau of government have not yet quit bruxelle only serve to increase the confusion and consternation the inhabitant in general wish well to the arm of napoleon but they know that the retreat of the english army must necessarily take place through they town that we troops will perhaps endeavor to make a stand and that the consequence will be terrible to the inhabitant from the house be liable to be burn or pillage by friend or foe all the baggage of we army and all the military bureau have receive order to repair and be now on they march to antwerp and the road thither be so covered and block up by waggon that the retreat of we army will be much impeded thereby probably my next letter may be date from a french prison bruxelle June 21 judge my friend of my astonishment and that of almost everybody in this city at the news which be circulate here early on the morning of the 19th and have be daily confirm viz that the french army have be completely defeat and be in full flight leave behind it 220 piece of cannon and all its baggage waggon and _ munitions de guerre _ i have not be able to collect all the particulars but you will no doubt hear enough of it for i be sure it will be _ say _ or _ sing _ by all the partisan of the british ministry and all the tory of the unite kingdom for month and year to come for further detail therefore i shall refer you to the gazette the follow however you may consider as a tolerably fair précis of what take place the attack begin on the 18th about ten o'clock -lrb- 16 -rrb- and rage furiously along the whole line but principally at hougoumont a large _ métairie _ on the right of we position which be occupy by we troops and from which all the effort of the enemy could not dislodge they the slaughter be terrible in this quarter from twelve o'clock till evening several desperate charge of cavalry and infantry be make on the rest of we line both side fight with the utmost courage and obstinacy and be prodigal of life in the extreme but it be generally suppose that we army must have succumb towards the evening have it not be for the arrival of bulow 's division of prussian follow closely by blucher and the rest of the army which have rally with uncommon celerity these move on the right flank of the french and decide the fortune of the day by a charge which be second by a general charge from the whole of the english line on the center and left of the french see themselves thus turn a panic it be say spread among the young guard of the french army and a cry of _ sauve qui peut nous sommes trahis _ spread like wildfire the flight become universal the old guard alone remain refuse quarter and perish like leonida and he spartan the prussian cavalry be fresh pursue the enemy all night _ l'épée dan les rein _ and it may be conceive from they previous disposition that they would not be very merciful to the vanquish indeed on the 15th it be say that the french be not very merciful to they it be like the combat of achille and hector no thought but rage and never cease strife till death extinguish rage and thought and life france will now call out to napoleon as augustus do to varus give I back my legion the loss on both side be very great but it must have be prodigious on the side of the french the whole ally army be in full pursuit several friend and acquaintance of mine perish in this battle viz lieut-general sir t picton colonel sir h elli and colonel morice June 22 this morning i go to visit the field of battle which be a little beyond the village of waterloo on the plateau of mont st jean but on arrival there the sight be too horrible to behold i feel sick in the stomach and be oblige to return the multitude of carcase the heap of wounded man with mangled limb unable to move and perish from not have they wound dress or from hunger as the ally be of course oblige to take they surgeon and waggon with they form a spectacle i shall never forget the wound both of the ally and the french remain in a equally deplorable state at hougoumont where there be a orchard every tree be pierce with bullet the barn be all burn down and in the court-yard it be say they have be oblige to burn upward of a thousand carcase a awful holocaust to the war-demon as nothing be more distressing than the sight of human misery when we be unable to silence it i return as speedily as possible to bruxelle with cowper 's line in my head : war be a game which be they subject wise king should not play at i hope this battle will at any rate lead to a speedy peace June 28 we have no other news from the ally army except that they be move forward with all possible celerity in the direction of paris you may form a guess of the slaughter and of the misery that the wound must have suffer and the many that must have perish from hunger and thirst when i tell you that all the carriage in bruxelle even elegant private equipage landaulet barouche and berline have be put in requisition to remove the wound man from the field of battle to the hospital and that they be yet far from be all bring in the medical practitioner of the city have be put in requisition and be order to make domiciliary visit at every house -lrb- for each habitation have three or four soldier in it -rrb- in order to dress the wound of the patient the bruxelloi the woman in particular have testify the utmost humanity towards the poor sufferer it be suggest by some humane person that they who go to see the field of battle from motive of curiosity would do well to take with they bread wine and other refreshment to distribute among the wound and most people do so for my part i shall not go a second time napoleon it be say narrowly escape be take he carriage fall into the hand of the ally and be escort in triumph into bruxelle by a detachment of dragoon so confident be napoleon of success that print proclamation be find in the carriage date from we imperial palace at laecken announce he victory and the liberation of belgium from the insatiable coalition and wherein he call on the belgian to re-unite with they old companion in arm in order to reap the fruit of they victory this be certainly rather premature and remind I of a anecdote of a spanish officer at the siege of gibraltar relate by drinkwater in he narrative of that siege -lrb- 17 -rrb- when the british garrison make a sortie they carry the advanced spanish line and destroy all they preparation the spanish officer on guard at the outermost post be kill but on the table of he guard room be find he guard report fill up and sign state that nothing extraordinary have happen since guard-mounting mr l of northumberland have propose to I to make a tour with he to aix-la-chapelle and the bank of the rhine i shall start -- return to bruxelle -- napoleon 's surrender liége June 26 mr l and myself start together in the diligence from bruxelle at seven o'clock in the evening of the 24th inst and arrive here yesterday morning at twelve o'clock i experienced considerable difficulty in procure a passport to quit bruxelle my name have be include in that of general wilson which he carry back with he to england we ambassador be absent and i be bandy about from bureau to bureau without success so that i begin at last to think that i should be necessitate to remain at bruxelle all my life when fortunately it occur to mr l that he be intimately acquaint with the english consul and he kindly undertake to procure I one and succeed on arrival here we put up at the _ pommelette d'or _ the price of a place in the diligence from bruxelle to liége be fifteen frank we pass thro ' louvain but too late to see anything the country about liége be extremely striking and picturesque the river meuse flow thro ' the city and the bank of the river outside the town be very _ riant _ and agreeable liége be a large well-built city but rather gloomy as to its appearance and lie in a hollow completely surround by lofty hill the remains of its ancient citadel stand on a height which completely command the city on another height stand a monastery a magnificent building there be a great many coal-pit in the vicinity of liége and a great commerce of coal be carry on between this city and holland by the _ treckschuyte _ on the meuse we visit the ancient episcopal palace and the church the palace be completely dismantle this city suffer much during the revolt of the belgian province against the emperor joseph ii and have distinguish itself by the obstinacy of its defense it be treat with great rigor by the austrian government the fortification be blow up and nothing now remain on the site of the old citadel but a large barrack i remain two whole hour on this height to contemplate the beauty of the expanse below the bank of the river which meander much in these part and the numerous _ maisons de campagne _ with the public promenade and allées line with tree exhilarate the scene of the environs for the city itself be dull enough several pretty villa be situate also on the height and be i to dwell here i should choose one of they and seldom descend into the valley and city below where narrow care and strife and envy dwell liége however sombre in its appearance be a place of much opulence and commerce a belgian garrison do duty here at the inn after dinner i fall into conversation with a belgian priest and as i be dress in black he fancy i be one of the cloth and he ask I if i be a belgian for that i speak french with a belgian accent apparemment monsieur est ecclésiastique -- monsieur je suis né anglais et protestant he then begin to talk about and declaim against the french revolution for that be the doctrine now constantly din into the ear of all those who take order and he conclude by say that thing would never go on well in europe until they restore to god the thing they have take from he i tell he that i differ from he very much for that the sale of the church domain and of the land and fund belong to the suppress ecclesiastical establishment have contribute much to the improvement of agriculture and to the comfort of the peasantry whose situation be thereby much ameliorate and that they be now in a state of affluence compare with what they be before the french revolution i add : enfin monsieur dieu n ' a pas besoin des chose terrestre on my say this he do not chuse to continue the conversation but call for a bottle of wine drink it all himself with the zest of a tartuffe i believe that he be surprise to find that a englishman should not coincide with he sentiment for i observe all the adherent of the ancient régime of feudality and superstition have a idea that we be anxious for the re-establishment of all those abuse as they themselves be and it must be confess that the conduct of we government have be such as to authorize they fully in form such conjecture and that we shall be they staunch auxiliary in endeavor to arrest and retrograde the progress of the human mind in fact i soon perceive that my friend be not overload with wit and that he be one of those priest so well describe by metastasio : il di cui sapere sta nel nostro ignorar maastricht 27th June this morning after a promenade on the bank of the meuse -- for i be fond of river and wood -lrb- _ flumina amo silvasque inglorius _ -rrb- -- we embark on a _ treckschuyt _ and arrive here after a passage of four hour the scenery on the bank of the meuse all the way from liége to maastricht be highly diversify and extremely romantic but here at maastricht this cease and the dull uniformity of the dutch landscape begin when on the rampart of the city to the north and west a immense plain as far as the eye can reach present itself to view a few tree and sandhill form the only relief to the picture the town itself be neat clean and dull like all dutch town the fortification be strong and well worth inspection the most remarkable thing in the neighborhood of maastricht be the montagne de st pierre which from have be much excavate for the purpose of procure stone form a labyrinth of a most intricate nature i advise every traveller to visit it and if he have a classical imagination he may fancy himself in the labyrinth of crete aix-la-chapelle 29th June we start in the morning of the 28th from maastricht in the diligence for aix-la-chapelle and arrive here at twelve o'clock put up at van gülpen 's hotel _ zum pfälzischen hofe _ -lrb- à la cour palatine -rrb- which i recommend as a excellent inn and the host as very good people the price of we journey from liége to maastricht in the water-diligence be 2-1\/2 frank and from maastricht to aix-la-chapelle by land be 7 frank the person the road from maastricht to this place be not very good but the country at a short distance from maastricht become picturesque much diversify by hill and dale and well wood as the meuse form the boundary between the belgic and prussian territory we enter the latter sooner after leave maastricht i find my friend l a most agreeable travel companion travel seem to be he passion as it be mine and fortune have so far favor I in this particular that my professional duty and private affair have lead I to visit the four quarter of the globe after dinner on the first day of we arrival here we go to visit the _ hôtel de ville _ before which stand on a pedestal in a bason a ancient bronze statue of charlemagne it have nothing to recommend it but its antiquity the _ hôtel de ville _ be similar to other gothic building use for the same purpose in the great hall thereof there be a large picture represent the ambassador of all the power who assist at the signing of the treaty of aix-la-chapelle in 1742 and a full length portrait of the present king of prussium as master of the city occupy the place where once stand that of napoleon its late lord we next go to see the cathedral and sit down on the throne on which the german caesar use to be crown we view likewise the various costly article of plate the gift of pious prince the most remarkable thing among they be several superb dress of gold and silver embroidery so thickly lay on that they be of exceed weight these dress form part of the wardrobe of the virgin mary next to be see be a case or chest of massy silver adorn with innumerable precious stone of great value which case contain the bone or ash of charlemagne he right arm bone be however preserved separate in a glass case the sword of this prince too and the imperial crown be to be see here the sacristan next proceed to show to we the other relic but have begin with the exhibition of a rag dip in the sweat of jesus christ and a nail of the holy cross we begin to think we have see enough and go away perfectly satisfied there be no other monument in honor of charlemagne but a plain stone on the floor of the church with the simple inscription carolo magno on go out of the city thro ' one of the gate and at a short distance from it we ascend the mountain or rather hill call the louisberg on which be build a ridotto and café as also a column erect in honor of napoleon with a suitable inscription the inscription be efface and be about to be replace by another in the german language in commemoration of the downfall of the _ tyrant _ as the coalition be pleased to call he this tyrant be however extremely regret by the inhabitant of aix-la-chapelle and not without reason for he be a great benefactor to they and continually embellish the city confirm and increase its privilege the inhabitant be not at all pleased with they new master for the behavior of the prussian military have be so insulting and overbearing towards the burgher and student that it be i be tell a common exclamation among the latter allude to the prussian have stile themselves they deliverer : _ de nostris liberatoribus domine libera no _ indeed i can evidently discern that they be not particularly pleased at the result of the battle of waterloo in the evening i go to the theater which have the most inconvenient form imaginable be a rectangle as anti-gallicanism be the order of the day only german drama be allow to be perform and this night it be the tragedy of faust or dr faustus as we term he in england not the faust of goethe which be not mean for nor at all adapt to the stage but a drama of that name write by klingmann -lrb- 18 -rrb- it be a strange wild piece quite in the german style and full of horror and diablery in this piece the sublime and terrible border close on the ridiculous for instance the devil and faust come to drink in a beer-schenk or ale-house ` tis true the devil be incognito at the time and be call der fremde or the stranger it be only towards the conclusion of the piece that he discover himself to be satan the actor who play the part of the stranger have something in he physiognomy very terrific and awe-inspiring in another scene which to we would appear laughable and absurd but which please a german audience three woman in mask come on the stage to meet faust in a churchyard and on unmask display three skeleton head poor faust have stipulate to give he soul to the devil for aid he in the attainment of he desire the devil on he part agree to allow he to commit four deadly sin before he shall call on he to fulfil he contract faust in the sequel kill he wife and he father-in-law satan then claim he faust plead in arrest of judgement that he have only committed two crime out of the four for which he have agree and that there consequently remain two other for he to commit before he could be claim the devil in rejoinder inform he that he wife be with child at the time he kill she which constitute the third crime and that the very act of make a contract with the devil for he soul form the fourth faust overwhelm with confusion have not a word to say and satan seize he by the hair of he head carry he off in triumph this piece be write in iambic of ten syllable and the versification appear to I correct and harmonious and the sentiment forcible and poetical this fully compensate for the bizarrerie of the story itself which by the bye with all the reproach throw by the adherent of the classic taste on those of the romantic be scarcely more _ outré _ than the introduction of death -lrb- -lrb- greek : _ thanato _
