depart from pari on the 24th June 1816 i vary my journey into switzerland this time for instead of travel thro ' lyon or dole i take the route of besangon pontarlier jougne and orbe the country between dijon and besançon be a rich and fertile plain at besançon the mountainous country begin it be a strong fortress and the last considerable town of the french frontier it lie in a very picturesque situation be nearly environ by the doub which meander under its wall and by very lofty mountain on the other side of the doub stand the citadel its chief strength the town of besangon be exceedingly handsome and well build and there be several agreeable promenade two of which i must particularize viz the promenade de chamarre and the garden of the palace of granvelle there be besides several roman antiquity and the remains of a large amphitheatre i amuse myself very well for a couple of day at besançon and meet with some agreeable society at the _ hôtel de france _ where i lodged i leave besançon at eight in the morning of the 30th June and arrive at pontarlier at six the same evening pontarlier be a dreary melancholy look place consist of a very long street and several offset of street situate in the midst of mountain eternally cover with snow winter reign here during nine month of the year at pontarlier the whole garrison be under arm when i arrive to pay the last duty to a most respectable and respected officer whose death be occasion by fall into the river while at the _ necessary _ by the under board give way this officer have serve in almost all the campaign of napoleon and have greatly distinguish himself what a cruel death for a warrior who have be in fifty battle that death should have shun he in the field of battle to make he fall in a manner at once inglorious and ridiculous yet such be destiny pyrrhus fall by a tile fling from a house by a old woman and i be acquaint with a gallant captain in the british navy who lose he leg by amputation have break it -lrb- oh horror -rrb- by a fall from the top of a stage coach i leave pontarlier on the 2d July and arrive at lausanne the same evening at five o'clock on my return to lausanne i have the pleasure to form a acquaintance with several eminent frenchman proscribe and banish from france on account of have vote the death of loui xvi as member of the national convention which try he and for have vote after the return of napoleon from elba the _ acte additionnel _ which exclude the bourbon for ever from the throne of france among they be 1st monsieur lamarque who be one of the commissioner send by the convention to arrest dumouriez but be seize by he and deliver over to the austrian he pass some time in captivity and be at length release by be exchange with some other against the duchess d'angoulême -lrb- 67 -rrb- he be a very able man and seem to have far more political talent than any of the other _ conventionnel _ who be here on napoleon 's return from elba he vote for he but make strong objection against the formation of a peerage which he say be perfectly useless in france and pregnant with mischief to boot as it would only serve as a _ appuus _ to despotism he write a pamphlet with some excellent remark on this subject he therein point out the evil of a hereditary chamber and of a priviledged aristocracy who have nothing to expect from the people but all from the prince and in its stead he propose a additional elective chamber something on the plan of the senate in america but he decidedly reprobate a hereditary peerage the next be general espinassy a very good classical scholar and a most upright and amiable man -lrb- 68 -rrb- in he vote he be solely influence by strong but conscienscious republican principle he reside here with he wife and two son he be consider as one of the best engineer officer in france and he oppose the nomination of napoleon to the imperial dignity in 1804 another m borda -lrb- 69 -rrb- oppose napoleon 's assumption of the consulship on the 18th brumaire and be proscribe by he for a short time but afterwards amnesty and receive into favor he give he vote for napoleon on the _ champ de mai _ in 1815 but accompany this vote by a bold speech towards napoleon wherein he find fault with he former despotic practise and remind he of the solemnity of he promise to govern in future paternally and nationally as become the sovereign of a free people m borda be a very cheerful lively companionable man and tho ' seventy year of age he have a uncommon share of vivacity with something of the _ ci-devant jeune homme _ about he and he be pleased to be consider still as a man _ à bonnes fortune _ the next to he be m gauthier who have be a lawyer and hold a considerable post as a magistrate in the time of the republic and under the empire -lrb- 70 -rrb- he possess a good deal of talent close logical reasoning and have determine public principle the next m michaud have be also a advocate and be possessor of considerable property in the department of the doub -lrb- 71 -rrb- he be a most rigid unbending republican something in the style of verrina in schiller 's _ fiesco _ he oppose the assumption of the supreme power by buonaparte on the 18th brumaire he vote against the consulship for life as well as against the assumption of the imperial dignity he be a very good classical scholar he be a widower and have with he here mlle elisa he only daughter who follow she father 's fortune she be a very amiable and accomplished young lady she have a thorough knowledge of music and of painting in oils and be classically verse in the italian language i soon become acquaint with the whole of these illustrious exile and i find great delight and instruction from they conversation and this be a great relief to I for the life one lead in a swiss town be rather monotonous lausanne i dine very often with my neighbor the baron de falkenskioeld and at he house i become acquaint with m de laharpe who be preceptor to the present emperor of russium he be a native of this canton and have return here to pass the remainder of he life he be marry to a very amiable russian lady and have acquire a pretty good fortune in russium he live here very happily and comfortably but notwithstanding this he be often tempt to visit paris milan and other great city and when there sigh to return to he native mountain as the ultra of france bear a great hatred towards the inhabitant of the canton de vaud on account of the asylum give and sympathy show to the _ proscrit _ they have be at the pain of trump up and print a pretended petition from the inhabitant of the department of the doub pray that the french government would endeavor to obtain the removal of these _ proscrit _ from the canton de vaud and state that the say canton be the _ foyer _ of jacobinical principle and the place where napoleon 's return from elba be plan and accelerate and thro ' which the conveyance of intelligence backwards and forwards be conduct i have no doubt that in this petition more be mean than meet the ear that the oligarch of bern as well as the ultra of france have a share in it and that it may be consider not so much as a attempt to compel the canton to refuse asylum to these exile as to excite the great power to enforce the abolition of the independence of vaud and to replace it under the dominion and authority of the canton of bern everybody here however see thro ' the drift of this petition and many person whose name be put down as have sign it have write to they friend at lausanne to declare not only that they never sign such a petition but they entire ignorance even of the agitation of the question till they see the petition itself in print the french government however have not venture to act any further upon it than to make a pompous display of the royalist zeal and _ bon esprit _ that pervade the department of the doub i see a good deal of mlle michaud i find she conversation extremely agreeable she have lend to I a italian work by verrus entitle _ le nottus romane al sepolcro di stipione _ she be a very rigid catholic have be educate by a priest of very strict idea she devotion however do not render she less cheerful or less amiable she have express a wish to hear the protestant church service i offer to accompany she and we go together one Sunday to the cathedral church at lausanne but it unfortunately happen that on that day a sermon be preach which must have give a great deal of pain to she filial feeling mr levade the minister take it into he head to give a political sermon in which after a great deal of commonplace abuse of voltaire rousseau and the french revolution and very fulsome adulation towards the english government -lrb- a subject which be bring in by the head and shoulder -rrb- of that _ island _ -lrb- as he term it -rrb- _ surround by the ocean _ he lavish a great deal of still more fulsome adulation on the bourbon and then most wantonly and unnecessarily begin a furious declamation against the _ régicides _ as he term they who have take refuge in the canton and intimate pretty plainly how please it would be to god almighty that they should be expel from it this intolerant discourse more worthy of a rave jesuit than of a protestant minister be deservedly scout by the inhabitant of lausanne but this do not hinder poor mlle michaud from be much affect at the opprobrious tirade direct against a set of man among whom she father bear a conspicuous part and who act from patriotic motive i must not omit to state that in this discourse m levade interweave some hyperbolical compliment towards the young prince of sweden who attend the service that morning he tell he that the eye of all europe be fix upon he and that providence have he under he especial care now the follow be the character of m levade -lrb- 72 -rrb- he be a time-serving meddling priest and a most flagrant adulator of the power that be he think that by declaim against the french revolution and against voltaire and rousseau that he will get into favor with the great people who pass thro ' lausanne with the french and english government adherent and with the great tory family of england no considerable personage ever pass through lausanne but mr levade be the first to make he a visit and no rich or noble english family arrive with whom he do not ingratiate himself and he be not spare of he adulation this mode of procedure have be a very profitable concern to he as he have receive a vast number of present and several valuable legacy besides secure a number of pupil among the english family that come or that have be here he be in short a thorough parasite and time server in every sense of the word this adulation of the bourbon family in he sermon besides the meanness of it be highly misplace come from the mouth of a protestant minister and somebody exclaim on leave the church : _ que doit-on penser d'un ministre protestant du canton de vaud qui prodigue des louanges à une famille qui a été l'ennemie acharnée de l'elise reformée et qui a persécuté les protestants d'une manière si atroce _ but mr levade -lrb- tho ' to the honor of the clergyman of the canton de vaud he be singular among _ they _ -rrb- yet he have many person who perfectly resemble he among the member of the church of england and who be as eager to support despotism and to crush liberty as any disciple of loyolum or any janissary of the grand signor the other protestant minister of this canton be highly indignant at this sermon in fact it be the first time in this city that the house of god have be profane by the introduction of political subject into a religious discourse this sermon be the common topic of conversation for many day after chambéry 2d August i leave lausanne for geneva on 28 July i stop at nyon to pay a visit to mme duthon with whom i become acquaint at paris i dine with she and pass a most agreeable day she talent be of the first order and she be as great a enthusiast for the german language and litterature as myself besides be well verse in italian she have a female relation with she we take a boat after dinner to navigate the lake and we visit the château and domain of joseph napoleon the next day i proceed to geneva i determine on make the journey into italy this time by mont-ceni and to make it on foot as far as the foot of mont-ceni on the italian side intend to profit of the opportunity of the first conveyance i should meet with at suza to proceed to turin i accordingly forward my portmanteau to turin to the care of a banker there and sally forth from geneva at six o'clock on the morning of 1st August i stop to dine at frangy and reach romilly at seven in the evening there be nothing worthy of remark at romilly the next morning i stop at aix to breakfast and visit the bath establishment the scenery be picturesque on this route and the whole road from aix to chambéry be align with remarkably fine large tree at three in the afternoon i arrive at chambéry the capital of savoy it be a large handsome city situate in a fruitful valley with a great many garden and orchard surround it there be a strong garrison here among the many _ maison de plaisance _ in the environs of this city the most distinguishable be the villa of general de boigne who have pass the greatest part of he life in indium in the service of scindiah one of the mahratta chief -lrb- 73 -rrb- and it be by de boigne 's assistance that scindiah from be a petty chief with not more than three or four hundred horse become the founder of a powerful kingdom comprize chiefly of the province of the gange and jumna tear from the mogol empire whose sovereign fall into the hand of scindiah scindiah cause the mogol emperor 's eye to be put out and keep he as a state prisoner in delhus till the year 1805 when on the mahratta engage in war with the english scindiah be defeat by lake and lose the greater part of he conquest de boigne have quit indium in 1796 long before this rupture take place and at that time scindiah have a fine regular army of thirty battalion of 1000 man each disciplined armed and equip in the european manner he have likewise sixty squadron of regular cavalry and a formidable train of artillery at chambéry i meet with two french _ voyageur de commerce _ who with that positiveness which be often the national characteristic insist that de boigne owe he riches and fortune to he treachery in have betray and sell tippoo saib to the english when he be in tippoo 's service and i find this be the current report all over savoy now it be a accusation totally devoid of foundation as i shall presently show and i take this opportunity of vindicate the reputation of de boigne by simply state that de boigne could never have betrayd tippoo since he be never in he service 2dly that he have when in the service of scindiah fight against tippoo when the mahratta coalesce with the english against that prince in 1792 and that have it not be for the assistance give by the mahratta to the english -lrb- a most impolitic coalition on the part of the mahratta as it turn out afterwards -rrb- tippoo would not have be compel to conclude so humiliating a treaty of peace 3dly that de boigne have quit indium in 1796 three year before the second war and death of tippoo in 1799 i state too that i be perfectly well acquaint with these particulars of de boigne 's career from have serve six year in indium and from have be personally acquaint with a gentleman of the name of lucius ferdinand smith who be the ultimate friend of de boigne and he lieutenant general in the service of scindiah i add that i could not conceive how so unjust and unfounded a aspersion on de boigne 's character could find currency i hope that what i say will be effectual towards do away this injurious report but very probably it will not for when the vulgar once imbibe a opinion it be difficult to eradicate it from they mind and they be not at all oblige to the person who endeavor to undeceive they so that general de boigne 's treachery and sale of tippoo to the english will be hand down to posterity among the savoyard as a fact of which it will be as little permit to doubt as of the treachery of juda chambéry August 3d at the _ table d'hôte _ this day i nearly lose all patience on hear a elderly english gentleman extol the english ministry to the sky and abuse the army of the loire call they rebel and traitor i stand up in defense of these gallant man and state that the french army in the time of the republic and of the empire be the most constitutional of all the european army since they be take from and identify with the people and that it be this brotherly feeling for they fellow citizen that induce they to join the standard of napoleon on he return from elba that they only follow the voice of the nation that all france be indignant at the tergiversation and breach of faith on the part of the restore government in a variety of instance and that have napoleon and the army be out of the question the bourbon would not have fail to be upset from the indignation they measure have excite among the people he then say that the army of the loire be a most dangerous body of man and that that be the reason why the ally insist on they be disband i reply that this be the highest compliment he could pay they and the greatest feather in they cap since it go to prove that as long as this army be in existence neither the crown despot nor the ultras thought themselves safe and that they could not venture to pursue they anti-national project which be all direct towards deprive the french people of all they have gain by the revolution and bring they back to the _ blessing _ of the ancient _ régime _ he could say nothing in reply but that he fear i have jacobin principle to which i make rejoinder : if these be jacobin principle i glory in they some sardinian officer who be present seem to enjoy my argument tho ' they say nothing and one take I aside when we quit the table and say he rejoice to see I take the old man in hand as he disgust they every day by he tirade against the liberal party and by he fulsome adulation of the british government the old gentleman hold forth likewise in a long speech respect the finances of england in praise of the sink fund and when it be suggest to he that england from the immense national debt must one day become bankrupt : _ non monsieur _ -lrb- he say -rrb- _ la caisse d'amortissement empêchera cela _ in fine the _ caisse d'amortissement _ be to work miracle i reply that the principle of the _ caisse d'amortissement _ be good provide a constant and consistent economy be practice but that at present and during the whole time from its establishment it have be a mockery on the understanding of the nation when we reflect on the profligate expenditure of public money occasion by the ruinous unjust and liberticide war which be enter into and foment by the british government indeed i say it be like the conduct of a man who possess a income of 200 # per annum should set apart in a box as a _ caisse d'épargne _ 20 # annually and at the same time continue a style of live the annual expence of which would so far exceed he income as to oblige he to borrow 7 or 800 # every year the old gentleman be all amort at this comparison which must be obvious to every one nothing show in a more glaring light the blind and superstitious reverence pay to great name for because this sink fund be propose by tuscan idiom and custom -- monument and gallery at florence -- the cascino -- church -- theater -- popularity of the grand duke -- napoleon 's downfall not regret -- academy in florence florence 26th August the moment you leave bologna to go to florence you enter the gorge of the appennine and after journey seven mile begin to ascend the ridge the ascent begin at pianoro among these mountain the scenery be wild and romantic and tho ' not so grandiose and sublime as that of the alp be nevertheless extremely picturesque one meet occasionally with the ruin of old castle on some of the height and i be strongly remind at the sight of these antique edifice of the mystery of udolpho and the time of the condottierus the silence that reign here be only interrupt by the noise of the waterfall and the occasional scream of the eagle the wild abrupt transition of landscape would suggest the idea of haunting place for robber yet one seldom or never hear of any on this road in tuscany there be i understand so much industry and morality that a robbery be a thing unknown but in he holiness 's dominion from the idleness and poverty that prevail they be say to be frequent why it do not occur in these mountain in that part of they at least which belong to the papal government i be at a loss to conceive here the chesnut and olive tree salute the ultramontane traveller for the first time the olive tree tho ' a most useful be not a ornamental one as it resemble a willow or osier in its trunk and in the color of its leave the chesnut tree be a glorious plant for a indolent people since it furnish food without labor as the xaca or jack fruit tree do to the cingalese in ceylon on one of the height between pianoro and lojano you have in very clear weather a view of both the adriatic and tyrrhene sea we bring to the night at scarica l'asino and the next morning early we enter the tuscan territory at pietra mala where there be a _ douane _ and consequently a examination of trunk at one o'clock we arrive at a inn call _ le maschere _ about fifteen mile distance from florence it be a large mansion and be situate on a eminence command a extensive view one become soon aware of be in the tuscan territory from the number of cultivate spot to be see in this part of the appennine : for such be the industry of the inhabitant that they do wonder on they naturally sterile soil one see a number of farm every spot of ground be in cultivation between _ le maschere _ and florence in particular these spot of ground garden orchard and villa form a striking and please contrast with the wild and dreary scenery of the appennine another thing that indicate one 's arrival among the tuscan be they aspiration of the letter _ c _ before _ a _ _ o _ and _ u _ which be at first extremely puzzling to a foreigner accustom only to the roman pronunciation for instance instead of _ camera _ _ cotto _ _ curvo _ they pronounce these word _ hamera _ _ hotto _ and _ hurvo _ with a exceed strong aspiration of the _ h _ it be the same too with the _ ch _ which they aspirate _ ex gr _ instead of _ pochino _ _ chiave _ they say _ pohino _ _ hiave _ the language however which be speak be the most classical and pure italian and except the above mention aspiration it be delightful to the ear peculiarly so to those who come from the north of italy and have only hitherto hear the unpleasing nasal twang of the milanese and the exceed uncouth barbarous dialect of bologna another striking peculiarity be the smart appearance of the tuscan peasantry they be a remarkably handsome race of man the female unite with they natural beauty a grace and elegance that one be quite astonished to find among peasant they express themselves in the most correct and classical language and they have a great deal of repartee as the peasantry of tuscany enjoy a greater share of _ aisance _ than fall to the lot of those of any other country and as the female dress with taste and take great pain to appear smart on all occasion they resemble rather the shepherdess on the opera stage or those of the fabled arcadium than anything in real life the female too be remarkably industrious and will work like horse all the week to gain wherewithal to appear smart on holiday they dress be very become and they wear sometimes jewellery to a large amount on they person a very common ornament among they be a collar of gold around they neck they usual head-dress be either a white straw hat or a black round beaver hat with black ostrich feather i prefer the straw hat it be more tasteful than the round hat which always seem to I too masculine for a woman at the inn at _ le maschere _ we be wait on by three smart female the whole road from _ le maschere _ to florence be very beautiful and diversified vineyard garden farm house and villa thicken as one approach and when arrive within three mile of florence which lie in a basin surround by mountain one be quite bewildered at the sight of the quantity of beautiful villa and _ maison de plaisance _ in every direction every thing indicate life industry and comfort in this charming country we stop at a villa belong to the grand duke call _ ii pratolino _ seven mile distant from florence here be to be see the famous statue represent the genius of the appennine the villa be unfurnished and out of repair and the garden and grounds be neglect persecution by they own intolerance whereas when they get the upper hand they show no mercy to those of a different religion and orthodoxy have wallow successively in the blood of arian jew moor and puzzuolus and baia -- theater at naple -- pulcinello -- return to rome -- tivolus i start from rome on the 26th September in the same _ vettura _ i find a intelligent young frenchman of the name of r -- d -- a magistrate in corsica who be travel in italy for he amusement there be besides a roman lawyer and not a very bright one by the bye and a fat woman who be go to naple to visit she lover a captain in the austrian service a large body of austrian troops be still at naple we issue from rome by the _ porta latina _ and reach albano -lrb- the ancient alba -rrb- sixteen mile distant at twelve o'clock we repose there two hour which give I a opportunity of visit the _ villa dorium _ where there be magnificent garden these garden form the promenade of the family who come to albano to pass the heat of the summer and to avoid the effect of the exhalation of the marshy country about rome as albano be situate on a eminence you have a fine view of the whole plain of latium and rome in perspective the country of latium however be flat dreary and monotonous it afford pasture to a immense quantity of black cattle such as buffalo etc just outside of albano on the route to naple be a curious ancient monument call _ il sepolcro deglus orazj e curiazj _ it be build of brick be extremely solid of singular appearance from its be a square monument flank at each angle by a tower in the shape of a cone it be of a uncouth rustic appearance and must certainly have be build before _ grecium capium ferum victorem cepit et artes intulit agresti latio _ -lrb- 93 -rrb- and i see no reason against its be the sepulchre of the horatius and curiatius particularly as it stand so near alba where the battle be fight but be this as it may there be nothing like faith in matter of antiquity the sceptic can have little pleasure the country on leave albano become diversified woody and picturesque near gensano be the beautiful lake of nemus and it be the spot feign by the poet as the scene of the amours of mar and rhea silvium near gensano also be the country residence of the sovereign pontiff call castel gandolfo la riccia the next place we pass thro ' be the ancient aricium mention in horace 's journey to brundusium we arrive in the evening at velletrus velletrus be a large town or rather city situate on a mountain to which you ascend by a wind road skirt a beautiful forest from the terrace of one of the _ palazzo _ here you have a superb view of all the plain below as far as the rock of circe comprehend the pontine marsh there be several very fine building at velletrus and it be remarkable as be the birthplace of augustus caesar there be a spacious _ piazza _ too on which stand a bronze statue of pope urban viii velletrus be twenty-eight mile from rome the next morning the 27th we start early so as to arrive by six o'clock in the evening at terracina at cisterna be a post-house and at torre tre pontus be a convent a beautiful building but now delapidate and neglect near it be a wretched inn where however you be always sure to find plenty of game to eat here begin the pontine marsh and the famous appian road which run in a right line for twenty-five mile across the marsh it be repair and perfectly reconstruct by pius vi and from he it bear its present appellation of _ linea pium _ this convent and church be also construct by pius vi with a view to facilitate the drain and cultivate of the marsh by afford shelter to the workman the _ linea pium _ be a very fine _ chaussée _ considerably raise above the level of the marsh well pave line with tree and a canal sink on one side to carry off the water the pontine marsh extend all the way from torre tre pontus to terracina on the left hand side on travel from rome to naple you have two mile or thereabout of plain bound by lofty mountain on the right a vast marshy plain bound by the sea at a distance of seven or eight mile nothing can be more monotonous than this strait road twenty-five mile in length and the same landscape the whole way the air be extremely damp aguish and unhealthy those who travel late in the evening or early in the morning be recommend not to let down the glass of the carriage in order to avoid inhale the pestilential miasma from the marsh which even the canal have not be able to drain sufficiently no one can find amusement in this desolate region but the sportsman and he may live in continual enjoyment and slay wild duck and snipe in abundance a number of buffalo be to be see grazing on the marsh they be not to be meet with to the north of rome they resemble entirely the buffalo of egypt and indium be black and they be very terrific look animal to the northern traveller who behold they here for the first time these marsh supply rome abundantly with waterfowl and other game of all kind every _ vetturino _ who be return to rome on pass by buy a quantity for a mere trifle from the peasantry who employ themselves much _ à la chasse _ and he be certain to sell they again at rome for three or four time the price he pay and even then it appear marvellous cheap to a englishman accustom as he be to pay a high price for game in he own country we arrive a little before six at terracina which be on the bank of the mediterranean and may be distinguish at a great distance by its white building the chain of mountain on the left of we road hither form a sort of arch to the chord of the _ linea pium _ and terminate one end of the arch by meet the _ linea pium _ at terracina which form what the sailor call a bluff point terracina stand on the situation of the ancient anxur and the description of it by horace in he brundusian journey impositum saxi late candentibus anxur -lrb- 94 -rrb- be perfectly applicable even now it be a handsome look city and be the last town in the pope 's territory : part of it be situate on the mountain and part on the plain at its foot close to the sea the fine white building on the height the temple of jupiter anxurus -lrb- of which the façade and many column remain entire -rrb- tower above they the orange tree and the sea afford a view doubly please and grateful to the traveller after the dreary landscape of the pontine marsh there be but one inn at terracina but that be a very large one there be however but very indifferent fare and bad attendance the innkeeper be a sad over-reaching rascal who fleece in the most unmerciful manner the traveller who be not _ spesato _ he be oblige to furnish those who be _ spesatus _ with supper and lodging at the _ vetturino 's _ price but he always grumble at it give the worst supper he can and bestow it as if he be give alms as the road between terracina and fondus -lrb- the first neapolitan town -rrb- be say to be at time infested by robber few traveller care to leave nor do we behold the sea again till just before we arrive at mola di gaeta which be a exceed long straggle town on its bank several fishing vessel lie here and it be here that part of the bay of the follow may be consider as a very fair specimen of the life of a young man of rank and fashion at naple he rise about two pm take he chocolate saunter about in the _ strada di toledo _ or in the _ do duty they be clothe in scarlet and _ à anglaise _ naple 8th octr one day i go to visit the museum or _ studius _ as it be call which be situate at the extremity of the _ strada di toledo _ on the be all the papyrus manuscript ' print _ visit to pompeius and ascent of vesuvius _ _ 11th oct _ we return mr r -- d -- and i from we visit to vesuvius half dead with fatigue from have have little or after have satisfy we curiosity here we regain the light of heaven in resina and proceed to pompeii which be seven mile further the total distance from naple to pompeii be ten mile the part of pompeius already discover look like a town with the house unroofed situate in a deep gravel or sand pit the depth of which be considerably greater than the height of the building stand in it you descend into it from the brink which be on a level with the rest of the country pompeius be consequently expose to the open air and you have neither to go under ground nor to use _ flambeau _ as at herculaneum but simply to descend as into a pit there be always a guard station at pompeius to protect the place from delapidation and theft of antiquarian from its resemble as i have already say a town in the center of a deep gravel pit you come upon it abruptly and on look down you be surprize to see a city newly bring to day the street and house here remain entire the roof of the house except which fall in by the effect of the excavation so that you here behold a roman city nearly in the exact state it be hi when it be bury under the ash of vesuvius during its first eruption in the year 79 of the christian era it do not appear to I that the catastrophe of pompeius could have be occasion by a earthquake for if so the street and house would not be find upright and entire : it appear rather to have be cause by the shower of ash and _ écroulement _ of the mountain which cover it up and bury it for ever from the sight of day the first place we guide take we to see be a superb amphitheatre about half as large as the coliseum : the arena and seat be perfect and all the interior be perfectly clear out : so be the den where the wild beast be keep so that you look down into this amphitheatre as into a vast basin standing on its brink which be on a level with the rest of the ground around it and by means of the seat and passage you may descend into the _ arena _ this amphitheatre be at a short distance from the rest of the town what be at present discover of this city consist of a long street with several off-set of street issue from it : a temple two theater a praetorium a large barrack and a peculiarly large house or villa belong probably to some eminent person but no doubt when the excavation shall be recommence many more street will be discover as from the circumstance of there be a amphitheatre two other theater and a number of sepulchral monument outside the gate it must have be a city of great consequence most of the house seem to have have two story the roof fall in of course by the act of excavation but the column remain entire i observe that the general style of building in pompeius in most of the house be as follow : that in each building there be a court yard in the center something like the court yard of a convent which be sometimes pave in mosaic and generally surround by column in the middle of this court be a fountain or basin : the court have no roof and the wing of the house form a quadrangle environ it the window and door of the room be make in the interior side of the quadrangle look into the court yard on the exterior there appear to be only a small latticed window near the top of the room to admit light i have see in egypt and in indium similarly build house and it be the general style of building in andalusium and barbary in the room be niche in the wall for lamp precisely in the style of the moorish building in indium in many of the chamber of the house at pompeius be painting _ al fresco _ and and fruit in other venus and the grace at they toilette from which we may infer that the former be dine room and the latter boudoir a large villa -lrb- so i deem it as it stand without the gate -rrb- have a number of room two story entire and three court yard with fountain many beautiful fresco painting on the wall of the chamber annex to this villa be a garden arrange in terrace and a fish pond a use cork instead of oil to stop they _ amphora _ wine eighteen hundred year old might have be find here it be not the custom even of the modern italian to use cork for the wine they keep for they own use : a spoonful of oil be pour on the top of the wine in the flask and when they mean to drink it they extract the oil by means of a lump of cotton fasten to a stick or long pin which enter the neck of the flask and absorb and extract the oil among the building discover in pompeius be a large temple of isis here you behold the altar and the pillar to which the beast of sacrifice be fasten in this city suppose to have be a barrack for soldier three skeleton be find here with they leg in a machine similar to we stock the scribble and caricature on the wall of this barrack be perfectly visible and legible when one wander thro ' the street of this singularly interesting city one be tempt to think that the inhabitant have just walk out what a dreadful linger death must have befall these inhabitant who could not escape from pompeius at the time of the eruption of vesuvius which cover it with ash the air could only be exhaust by degree so that a prolonged suffocation or a death by hunger must have be they lot four skeleton be find upright in the street have in they hand box contain jewellery and thing of value as if in the act of endeavor to make they escape : these must soon have perish but the skeleton of a woman find in one of the room of the house close to a bath shew that she death must have be one of prolonged suffer what a fine subject pompeius would furnish for the private life and manners of the ancient but if they have be leave here they would even tho ' a guard of soldier be station here to protect they have be by degree all steal there be some magnificent tomb just outside the gate which must have be no small ornament to the city we return to resina to dinner at six o'clock we have make a arrangement with one of the guide of vesuvius call salvatore that he should be ready for we at resina at seven o'clock with a mule and driver for each of we to ascend the mountain and we find he very punctual at the door of the inn at that hour the term of the journey be as follow one _ scudo _ for salvatore and one _ scudo _ for each mule and driver for which they be to forward we to the mountain remain the whole night and reconduct we to resina the follow morning the object in ascend at night and remain until morning be to combine the night view of the eruption with the visit -lrb- if possible -rrb- to the crater which can not with safety be undertake by night and to enjoy likewise the noble view at sunrise of the whole bay and city of naple and the adjacent island we start therefore at a quarter past seven and arrive at half past nine at a small house and chapel call the hermitage of vesuvius which be generally consider as half-way up the mountain in this house dwell a old ecclesiastic who receive traveller and furnish they with a couch and frugal repast we dismount here and we worthy host provide we with some mortadellum and a omelette and we do not fail to do justice to he excellent _ lacrima christus _ of which he have always a large provision we then betake ourselves to rest leave order to be awaken at two o'clock in order to proceed further up the mountain there be a pretty decent eruption of the mountain which vomit fire stone and ash at a interval of twenty-five minute so that we enjoy this spectacle during we ascent a violent noise like thunder accompany each eruption which increase the awefulness and grandeur of the sight at two o'clock we guide and muleteer be very punctual we bid adieu to the hermit promising he to come to breakfast with he the next morning we then mount we mule and after a hour 's march arrive at the spot where the ash and cinder combine with the steepness of the mountain prevent the possibility of go any further except on foot we dismount therefore at this place and send back we mule to the hermitage to wait for we there we now begin to climb among the ash and tho ' the ascent to the position of the ancient crater be not more than probably eighty yard in height we be at least one hour before we reach it from its excessive steepness and from glide back two foot out of three at every step we make we at length reach the old crater and sit ourselves down to repose till day-break tho ' it be exceed cold the exhalation from the vein of fire and hot ash keep we as warm as we could wish : for here every step be literally _ per igne supposito cineri doloso _ -lrb- 97 -rrb- we remain on this spot till broad daylight and witness several eruption at a interval of twenty or twenty-five minute i remark that the mountain toward the summit form two cone one of which vomit fire and smoke and the other calcined stone and ash accompany by a rumble noise like thunder the stone come clatter down the flank of the mountain and some of they roll very near we have we be within the radius form by the erupt stone we probably should have be kill at daylight mr r -- d -- propose to ascend the two cone in spite of the remonstrance of we guide salvatore who tell we that no person have yet be there and that we must expect to be crush to death by the stone should a eruption take place and that it be almost as much madness to attempt it as it would be to walk before a battery of cannon in the act of be fire tho ' i do not admit all the force of this comparison yet i begin to think there be a little too much risk in the attempt my french friend however be deaf to all remonstrance and say to I _ as-tu peur _ i reply : no that i be at all time very indifferent as to life or death but that i do not like pain and be not at all desirous to have a arm or leg break the former accident have happen to a german a few day before nevertheless i add if you persist in go i will accompany you we accordingly start to ascend the cone which vomit fire and smoke take care to place ourselves on the windward side in ascend and after much fatigue we arrive in about fifteen minute close to the apex of the cone after grope amidst the ash and stumble on a vein of red hot cinder my shoe be sadly burnt my stocking singe and my foot scorch my friend be less fortunate for he tumble down with he hand on a vein of red hot cinder and burn they terribly my great and principal apprehension in make this ascent be of stumble upon hole slightly encrust with ash and that the whole might give way and precipitate I into some _ gouffre _ on arrival at the summit of the cone we have just time to look down and perceive that there be a hole or _ gouffre _ but whether it be very deep or not we could not ascertain for a blast of fire and smoke issue from it at this moment nearly suffocate we we immediately lose no time in glide down the ash on the side of the cone on we breech and reach its base in a few seconds where we wait till a eruption take place from the other cone in order to profit of the interval to ascend it also it require four minute ' walk to reach the base of the other cone and about twelve to ascend to its apex on arrival at the brink where we remain about two minute we have just sufficient time to observe that there be no deep hole or bottomless _ gouffre _ as we expect but that it form a crater with a sort of slant and not exceed thirty foot in depth to the bottom which look exactly like a lime-kiln being of a dirty white appearance and in continual agitation as it be of limestone boil so that a person descend to the bottom of this crater would probably be scorch to death or suffocate in a few minute but would infallibly be eject and throw into the air at the first eruption i mean by this that he would not disappear or fall into a bottomless pit -lrb- as i should have suppose before i view the crater -rrb- but that he friend would be sure of find he body either yet live or dead outside the brink of the crater within the radius make by the erupt stone and ash we guide now beg we for god 's sake to descend as a eruption might be expect every minute we accordingly glide down the exterior surface of the cone among the ash on we breech for it be impossible to descend in any other way and in a few seconds we reach its base find ourselves on a little level ground we begin to run or rather wade thro ' the ash in order to get out of reach of the eruption but we have not go thirty yard when one take place the stone clatter down with a frightful noise and we receive a shower of ash on we head the dust of which get into we eye and nearly blind we on reach the brink of the old crater we stop half a hour to enjoy the fine view of parthenope in all she glory at sunrise we then descend rapidly sometimes plunge down the ash on we foot and sometimes glide on we breech till we arrive at the place where we have descend from we mule and this distance which require one hour to ascend cost we in its descent not more than seven minute we then walk to the hermitage in about a hour and a quarter and arrive there with no other accident than have we shoe and stocking totally spoil we foot a little singe the hand of mr rd severely burn and both begrime with ash like blacksmith the ecclesiastic give we a breakfast of coffee and egg and a glass of maraschino and we give he two _ scudus _ each before we depart he present to we he album which he usually do to all traveller invite they to write something i take up the pen and feel a little inspiration write the follow line : anch ` io salito son sul gran vesuvio mentre cadsa di cineri un diluvio questo cammin mi piace d'aver fatto ma plù mi piace il ritornare intatto which please the old man very much to see a foreigner write italian verse i pleased he still more by let he know that i be a enthusiastic admirer and humble cultivator of the tuscan muse and that have read and study most of they poet particularly _ il divino ariosto _ i now and then catch a _ scintilletta _ from he verse we now take a cordial farewell of we worthy old host mount we mule and descend the mountain on arrival at porticus we dismiss we guide salvatore with a _ scudo pour boire _ besides the stipulate price salvatore ask I to give he a write certificate of he service which he generally sollicit from all those whom he conduct to the volcano i ask he for he certificate book and beg to know whether he would have it in prose or verse he laugh and say : _ vostra excellenza è padrone _ i take out my pencil and write the follow quatrain : dal monte ignivomo tornati siam stanchissimi e del buon salvator siam tutti contentissimi felice il pellogrin che a salvator si fida che di lui non si può trovare un miglior guida i never see any body so delighted as salvatore appear when i read to he what i have write in he book i have another observation to make before i take leave of this celebrated mountain which be that the liquid lava which it eject be far more dangerous and destructive than the eruption of stone and ash the lava flow from the flank of the mountain in a liquid stream sometimes there will be a eruption and no lava flow : at other tune the lava flow from the flank of the mountain without any eruption from the crater at other time and then it be most alarming the eruption take place accompany by the flow of the lava all this demonstrate that the volcano be the effect of the effort of the subterraneous fire to get some vent and escape from its confinement this time i do not observe any lava flow except a slight vein of it on the spot where mr rd fall down and burn he hand but it be easy to observe on the side of the mountain the course and route take at different time by the lava which have become hardened and be very plainly to be distinguish as it resemble a _ river _ -lrb- if i may use the word -rrb- of slate meander between the green sward of the mountain and descend toward the sea you can plainly distinguish the course and direction of the lava which destroy part of torre del greco and sweep it into the sea at porticus have wash ourselves at the inn from head to foot in order to get rid of we blacksmith 's appearance and have purchase a new pair of shoe and stocking each we visit the royal palace and museum with a view principally of examine the object of art and valuable discover in pompeius the royal palace be call _ la favorita _ its architecture course pass through the grotto of pausilippo this grotto be thirty foot high and about five hundred foot long in fact it be a vast rock undermine and a high road run thro ' it the breadth of which be sufficient for three carriage to go abreast from its great length it be of course exceed dark in order therefore to obviate this inconvenience lamp constantly light be suspend from the roof and on the form between cape margelina and puzzuolus we stop at the lake agnano which be strongly impregnate with sulfur on the bank of this lake be the _ thermae _ or vapor bath and here be also the famous _ grotto del cane _ the pestilential vapor arise from which rise about three inch from the ground and have the appearance of a spider 's web a unfortunate dog perform the miracle of the resurrection to all those who visit this natural curiosity and we also be curious to see its effect the guardian of the therme seize the poor animal and hold he nose close to the place from whence the vapor exhale the dog be seize with strong convulsion and in two minute he be perfectly senseless and to all appearance dead but on be place in the open air he soon recover the poor beast shew evident repugnance to the experiment and i wonder he do not endeavor to make he escape for he have sometimes to perform this feat four or five time a day i should suppose that he will not be very long live for the repeat dose of this mephitic vapor must surely accelerate he dissolution the heat of the _ thermae _ and steam of the sulphur be almost insupportable but it have a most beneficial effect on malady of the nerve and cutaneous complaint we then proceed on we journey to puzzuoli the ancient puteolus where be the remains of the famous mole -lrb- or bridge as other call it -rrb- of caligulum intend to this descent really be fit to give one a idea of the descent to the shades below and what add to the illusion be that when we arrive at the bottom of the descent and just at the entrance of the cave -rrb- to return to puzzuoli by cross the bay at once in this bay and near cape misenus a roman fleet be usually station and pliny 's uncle i believe command one there at the time of the first eruption at the _ teatro nuovo _ i have see another italian tragedy perform the piece be _ tito manlio torquato _ take from the well know anecdote in the roman history the scenery decoration and _ costume _ be good and appropriate not so the act for the actor as usual be imperfect in they part i fully agree with alfierus that italy must be unite and enjoy a free popular government before one can expect to see tragedy well perform it be very divert to see the puppet show at naple and to hear the witticism and various artifice of the showman of pulcinello to secure payment in advance from he audience who would otherwise go away without pay as soon as the performance be over this performance be much attend by the _ lazzaronus _ and _ fainéans _ of the lower order of naple and the puppet showman be oblige to have recourse to various stratagem and ingenious sally to induce a handsome contribution to be make sometimes he will say with a very grave face -lrb- the curtain be draw up and no pulcinello appear -rrb- that he be very sorry there can be no performance this day for that poor signor pulcinello be sick and have no money to pay the doctor : but that if a _ quête _ be make for he he will get himself cure and make he appearance as usual all the while that one of the showman go about collect the _ granus _ the other hold a dialogue with pulcinello -lrb- still invisible -rrb- pulcinello groan and be very miserable at length the collection be make pulcinello take medicine say he be well again make he appearance and begin at another time the audience be inform that there can be no performance as pulcinello be arrest for debt and put in prison where he must remain unless a subscription of money be make for he to pay he debt and take he out of gaol then follow a absurd dialogue between pulcinello -lrb- suppose to answer from the prison -rrb- and the showman the showman scold he for be a spendthrift and lead a profligate life call he a _ briccone _ a _ birbante _ and pulcinello only groan out in reply _ povero I povero pulcinello che disgrazium sventurato di I di non aver denarus _ these stroke of wit never fail to bring in many a _ grano _ at another time the curtain be draw up and discover a gibbet and pulcinello standing on a ladder affix to it with a rope round he neck the showman with the utmost gravity and assume melancholy inform the audience that a most serious calamity be about to happen to naple : that signor pulcinello be condemn to be hang for a robbery and that unless he can procure _ moltus denari _ to bribe the officer of justice to let he escape he will inevitably be hang and the people will never more behold they unhappy friend pulcinello the showman now implore the commiseration of the audience and now reproach pulcinello with he profligacy and nefarious prank which have bring he to a untimely end pulcinello sob cry promise to reform and to attend mass regularly in future what neapolitan heart can resist such a appeal the _ granus _ be collect pulcinello give money to the puppet represent the executioner down go the gibbet and pulcinello be himself again i shall return in a day or two to rome have see nearly all that naple afford i have now full liberty to die when i chuse accord to the proverb : _ veder napolus e poí morire _ naple certainly be take it all in all the most interesting city in europe for it unite every thing that be conducive to the _ agrémens _ of life a beautiful city a noble bay a vast commerce provision of the best sort abundant and cheap a pleasant society a delicious climate music opera _ ballus _ library museum of painting and sculpture in its neighborhood two subterraneous city a volcano in full play and every spot of ground convey the most interesting _ souvenir _ and immortalize in prose and verse add thereto the vapor bath of sulphur for string anew the nerve of those debilitate by a too ardent pursuit of pleasure and the fountain of st lucium for those suffer from a redundancy of bile now tell I of any other residence which can equal this adieu rome 22nd octr nothing material occur on my return from naple to rome but on the 2d day after my arrival i make a excursion to tivolus which be about eighteen mile distant from rome i pass the night at the only inn at tivolus the next morning i walk to the _ villa d'este _ in this neighborhood which be a vast edifice with extensive grounds here on a terrace in front of the villa be model in marble of all the principal edifice and monument ancient and modern of rome very ingeniously execute from the _ villa d'este _ be a noble view of the whole plain of latium and of the eternal city from hence i walk about two mile further to visit the greatest antiquity and curiosity of the place which be the villa or rather the ruin of the celebrated villa build by adrian which must have be of immense size from the vast space of ground it occupy it be intend to unite everything that the magnificent idea of a prince could devise who wish to combine every sort of recreation sensual as well as intellectual within the precinct of he palace column frieze capital entablature and various other spoil of rich architecture cover the ground in profusion : many of the wall and archway be entire and almost a entire cupola remain standing besides the building above ground here be cellar under ground intend as quarter for the guard and capable of hold three thousand man as well as stable for horse in the inclosure of and form part of this villa which cover a circumference of seven mile be a gymnasium bath temple a school of philosopher tank a theater & c the greatest part of these building be choak up and cover with earth since it be by excavation alone that what do appear be bring to light it be by excavation that a man discover a large hall wherein he find the nine beautiful statue of the muse which now adorn the museum of the vatican and no doubt if the roman government would recommence the excavation many more valuable might be find hadrian 's villa have already furnish many a statue column and pilaster to the museum church and palace of rome i be much more gratify in behold the remains of this villa than in visit tivolus and i remain here several hour at four o'clock in the afternoon i start on my return to rome it be imprudent not to have start sooner as it be always dangerous to be outside the wall of rome after dark in consequence of the brigand who infest the environs and sometimes come close to the wall of the city i reach my hotel in rome at nine o'clock one hour and half after dark but have the good fortune to meet nobody the roman peasantry generally go armed and those who feed cattle in the field of the campagna or have any labor to perform there never sleep there
